                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Echinochloa crus-galli genome analysis provides insight into its adaptation and invasiveness as a weed"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2017 8:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-10-18"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Barnyardgrass (Echinochloa crus-galli) is a pernicious weed in agricultural fields worldwide. The molecular mechanisms underlying its success in the absence of human intervention are presently unknown. Here we report a draft genome sequence of the hexaploid species E. crus-galli, i.e., a 1.27&#8201;Gb assembly representing 90.7% of the predicted genome size. An extremely large repertoire of genes encoding cytochrome P450 monooxygenases and glutathione S-transferases associated with detoxification are found. Two gene clusters involved in the biosynthesis of an allelochemical 2,4-dihydroxy-7-methoxy-1,4-benzoxazin-3-one (DIMBOA) and a phytoalexin momilactone A are found in the E. crus-galli genome, respectively. The allelochemical DIMBOA gene cluster is activated in response to co-cultivation with rice, while the phytoalexin momilactone A gene cluster specifically to infection by pathogenic Pyricularia oryzae. Our results provide a new understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying the extreme adaptation of the weed. Barnyardgrass is a problematic agricultural weed. Here, via genomic analysis, Guo et al. identify genes potentially underlying its success, including a gene cluster activated in response to co-cultivation with rice that leads to synthesis of the allelochemical DIMBOA."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-5180-0348"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-5180-0348</a></span><sup             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Barnyardgrass (<i>Echinochloa crus-galli</i>) is a pernicious weed in agricultural fields worldwide. The molecular mechanisms underlying its success in the absence of human intervention are presently unknown. Here we report a draft genome sequence of the hexaploid species <i>E. crus-galli</i>, i.e., a 1.27âGb assembly representing 90.7% of the predicted genome size. An extremely large repertoire of genes encoding cytochrome P450 monooxygenases and glutathione S-transferases associated with detoxification are found. Two gene clusters involved in the biosynthesis of an allelochemical 2,4-dihydroxy-7-methoxy-1,4-benzoxazin-3-one (DIMBOA) and a phytoalexin momilactone A are found in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome, respectively. The allelochemical DIMBOA gene cluster is activated in response to co-cultivation with rice, while the phytoalexin momilactone A gene cluster specifically to infection by pathogenic <i>Pyricularia oryzae</i>. Our results provide a new understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying the extreme adaptation of the weed.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Agronomically important weeds cause tremendous worldwide losses of crop yields estimated at over $95 billion annually according to United Nations Food and Agriculture Organization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Lundkvist, A. &amp; Verwijst, T. (ed. Nokkoul, R.) Research in Organic Farming 157â186 (InTech, 2011)" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1048">1</a></sup>. Within the short period of agricultural history, weedy plants have exploited the human-mediated environment of farmersâ fields by undergoing rapid adaptive evolution that allowed them to proliferate and escape eradication using weediness traits such as rapid growth rates, prolonged dormancy, ease of dispersal, herbicide resistance, etc<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Vigueira, C. C., Olsen, K. M. &amp; Caicedo, A. L. The red queen in the corn: agricultural weeds as models of rapid adaptive evolution. Heredity &#xA;                           110, 303â311 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1052">2</a></sup>. Thus, weeds have evolved to survive in agroecosystems under natural and unintentionally human-mediated selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Warwick, S. I. &amp; Stewart, C. N. Crop Ferality and Volunteerism 9â30 (CRC, 2005)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1056">3</a></sup>, leading to a distinct evolutionary state that has proceeded in parallel to crop domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Vigueira, C. C., Olsen, K. M. &amp; Caicedo, A. L. The red queen in the corn: agricultural weeds as models of rapid adaptive evolution. Heredity &#xA;                           110, 303â311 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1060">2</a></sup>. Therefore, agricultural weeds are ideal models for the study of environmental adaption of plants from an evolutionary perspective. Beyond purely academic interests, uncovering how weeds evolve is crucial for their management and protection of the global food supply.</p><p>In contrast to numerous food and forage crops where the genomes of many important domesticated forms and their wild relatives have been sequenced leading to a better understanding of the genetic basis of many important agronomic traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Michael, T. P. &amp; VanBuren, R. Progress, challenges and the future of crop genomes. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. &#xA;                           24, 71â81 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1067">4</a></sup>, little genomic scale analysis has taken place on agricultural weed species despite their economic significance. In fact, among the important agricultural weeds known, only the dicot horseweed (<i>Conyza canadensis</i>) has had its whole genome sequenced thus far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Peng, Y. et al. De novo genome assembly of the economically important weed horseweed using integrated data from multiple sequencing platforms. Plant. Physiol. &#xA;                           166, 1241â1254 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1074">5</a></sup>.</p><p>The genus <i>Echinochloa</i> (Poaceae) or barnyardgrass includes approximately 250 annual and perennial species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bajwa, A. A. et al. Eco-biology and management of Echinochloa crus-galli. Crop. Prot. &#xA;                           75, 151â162 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1084">6</a></sup>. Systematic frequency assessments for rice weeds based on phytosociological studies showed that <i>Echinochloa</i> species are the most frequent weeds associated with rice cultivation globally<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kraehmer, H., Jabran, K., Mennan, H. &amp; Chauhan, B. S. Global distribution of rice weeds A review. Crop. Prot. &#xA;                           80, 73â86 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1091">7</a></sup>. Among <i>Echinochloa</i> species, <i>E. crus-galli</i> (annual, 2<i>n</i>â=â6<i>x</i>â=â54) is the most prevalent species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Aoki, D. &amp; Yamaguchi, H. Genetic relationship between Echinochloa crus-galli and Echinochloa oryzicola accessions inferred from internal transcribed spacer and chloroplast DNA sequences. Weed Biol. Manag &#xA;                           8, 233â242 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1108">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Yabuno, T. Biosystematic study of the genus Echinochloa. Jpn J. Bot. &#xA;                           19, 277â323 (1966)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1111">9</a></sup>, followed by <i>Echinochloa colona</i> and the tetraploid <i>Echinochloa oryzicola</i>
                        <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kraehmer, H., Jabran, K., Mennan, H. &amp; Chauhan, B. S. Global distribution of rice weeds A review. Crop. Prot. &#xA;                           80, 73â86 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1122">7</a></sup>. While conscious artificial selection for useful agronomical traits drove changes in early rice species during domestication, wild barnyardgrass growing in the same paddy fields was driven by natural and unintentional selections to evolve adaptive and competitive characteristics to evade removal from rice fields. For example, <i>E. crus-galli</i> in paddy fields morphologically resembles rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i>) at the seedling stage, which increase the difficulty of recognition during manual weeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Barrett, S. C. H. Crop Mimicry in Weeds. Econ. Bot. &#xA;                           37, 255â282 (1983)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1132">10</a></sup>. More recently, <i>E. crus-galli</i> acquired or evolved target-site and non-target-site herbicide resistance making it among the most pernicious herbicide resistant weeds of the world<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Norsworthy, J. K., Wilson, M. J., Scott, R. C. &amp; Gbur, E. E. Herbicidal activity on acetolactate synthase-resistant barnyardgrass (Echinochloa crus-galli) in Arkansas, USA. Weed Biol. Manag. &#xA;                           14, 50â58 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1139">11</a></sup>. Worldwide losses of yield in rice due to <i>E. crus-galli</i> competition are estimated to be about 35%<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Oerke, E. C. &amp; Dehne, H. W. Safeguarding production-losses in major crops and the role of crop protection. Crop. Prot. &#xA;                           23, 275â285 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1147">12</a></sup>.</p><p>Allelopathy, or the ability of one plant to suppress the growth of another nearby plant through the release of chemical compounds (i.e., allelochemicals) in the rhizosphere is one of the most important weediness features. Allelopathy plays an important role in natural and agricultural ecosystems, particularly in crop ecosystems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jabran, K., Mahajan, G., Sardana, V. &amp; Chauhan, B. S. Allelopathy for weed control in agricultural systems. Crop. Prot. &#xA;                           72, 57â65 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1154">13</a></sup> and has received much attention in recent years because it could potentially serve as an alternative weed management strategy in crop production without environmental cost<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Khanh, T., Xuan, T. &amp; Chung, I. Rice allelopathy and the possibility for weed management. Ann. Appl. Biol. &#xA;                           151, 325â339 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1158">14</a></sup>. Many field and greenhouse studies have demonstrated the allelopathic potential of rice cultivars on barnyardgrass<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Khanh, T., Xuan, T. &amp; Chung, I. Rice allelopathy and the possibility for weed management. Ann. Appl. Biol. &#xA;                           151, 325â339 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1162">14</a></sup>.</p><p>A number of plant secondary metabolites have been suggested to serve as allelochemicals and molecular genetic studies have shown that gene clusters are often responsible for the biosynthesis of the allelopathic compounds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Boycheva, S., Daviet, L., Wolfender, J. &amp; Fitzpatrick, T. B. The rise of operon-like gene clusters in plants. Trends Plant. Sci. &#xA;                           19, 447â459 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1170">15</a></sup>. The first allelochemical gene cluster intensively studied was that involved in the formation of 2,4-dihydroxy-7-methoxy-1,4-benzoxazin-3-one (DIMBOA), a protective and allelopathic defense compound against weeds, present in maize (<i>Zea mays</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Frey, M., Schullehner, K., Dick, R., Fiesselmann, A. &amp; Gierl, A. Benzoxazinoid biosynthesis, a model for evolution of secondary metabolic pathways in plants. Phytochemistry. &#xA;                           70, 1645â1651 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1177">16</a></sup>. The maize DIMBOA gene cluster is located on Chromosome 4 and consists of eight genes (<i>BX1</i> to <i>BX8</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Frey, M., Schullehner, K., Dick, R., Fiesselmann, A. &amp; Gierl, A. Benzoxazinoid biosynthesis, a model for evolution of secondary metabolic pathways in plants. Phytochemistry. &#xA;                           70, 1645â1651 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1188">16</a></sup>. In rice, two gene clusters involved in the biosynthesis of the allelopathic diterpene phytoalexins momilactone A and phytocassane have been identified on Chromosomes 4 and 2, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Shimura, K. et al. Identification of a biosynthetic gene cluster in rice for momilactones. J. Biol. Chem. &#xA;                           282, 34013â34018 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1192">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Swaminathan, S., Morrone, D., Wang, Q., Fulton, D. B. &amp; Peters, R. J. CYP76M7 is an ent-cassadiene C11Î±-hydroxylase defining a second multifunctional diterpenoid biosynthetic gene cluster in rice. Plant. Cell. &#xA;                           21, 3315â3325 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1195">18</a></sup>. These diterpene phytoalexins can significantly inhibit growth of <i>E. crus-galli</i>.</p><p>Considerable attention has been devoted to understand allelopathic interactions between crops and weeds, but most studies have focused on the genetic mechanisms of the impact of crops on weeds. Knowledge of the underlying basis for how weeds respond to the allelopathic challenge from crops and why weeds are dominant in crop fields without human intervention is limited. In this study, we generated a draft genome assembly for the hexaploid species <i>E. crus-galli</i>, the dominant weed in rice paddy fields, and analyzed the dynamic changes in transcriptome profiles of <i>E. crus-galli</i> growing alone and in response to the co-cultivation with rice plants. In the genome, We have identified three copies of gene clusters involved in production of an allelochemical (DIMBOA) used by <i>E. crus-galli</i> against rice and one copy of a phytoalexin (momilactone A) gene cluster against blast disease (<i>P. oryzae</i>) in the paddy field environments.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>The <i>E. crus-galli</i> line STB08, collected from rice paddy fields in the lower Yangtze River region of China, highly resembles cultivated rice in morphology (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">1</a>), and has a chromosome number of 2<i>n</i>â=â6<i>x</i>â=â54. A total of 207.4âGb of sequence data were generated using the Illumina HiSeq 2000 system from STB08 genomic DNA libraries with fragment sizes varying between 160âbp to 20âKb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">1</a>). In addition, the Pacbio RS II system was used to generate 32.9âGb third-generation long reads, totally representing â~â171Ã coverage of the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome estimated to be â~â1.4âGb in size based on the <i>K</i>-mer analysis and flow cytometry (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">2</a>). <i>De novo</i> assembly yielded a draft genome of 1.27âGb, representing 90.7% of the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome (â&gt;â1âKb), with a scaffold N50 length of 1.8âMb. Five fosmid clones (â&gt;â15âKb) were sequenced and compared with the assembly, and is confirm to be of good consistence (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">2</a>). About 92.3% of the core eukaryotic genes (CEGs) could be completely aligned with the <i>E. crus-galli</i> gene set. We have also used BUSCO to judge the assembly of <i>E. crus-galli</i>, and found that the âcompleteâ percent is 95.5%, which is comparable to that of <i>S. bicolor</i> (96.4%) and <i>S. italica</i> (94.3%) genome (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">3</a>). In addition, we mapped 74 publicly available <i>E. crus-galli</i> EST sequences and 156,757 transcripts generated by this study to the assembled genome and observed high mapping and identity rates (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the genome assembly and annotation of <i>E. crus-galli</i>
                                    </b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>For gene annotation, transcriptomic data from the whole plant were generated by RNA-Seq (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">4</a>). By integrating gene finding results from <i>ab initio</i>, homology- and transcript-based approaches, we predicted 108,771 protein-coding in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Tab1">1</a>). Of the 108,771 genes, 85% were supported by either the identification of homologues in other species or RNA-Seq data. In addition to protein-coding genes, 785 microRNAs (miRNAs) and other non-coding RNAs were also identified in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">5</a>). On the basis of homology searches and <i>de novo</i> methods, we identified a total of 514âMb of repetitive elements which represents 40.7% of the genomic assembly (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Tab1">1</a>). Among the repetitive sequences, long terminal retrotransposons (LTRs) were the most abundant, accounting for 21.9% of the assembly (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>Using pairwise protein sequence comparisons, putative orthologs and paralogs are analyzed among <i>E. crus-galli</i> and five other grass family members, including <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>, <i>Z. mays</i>, and <i>S. italica</i>. We found 26,679 gene families (containing a total of 72,363 genes) in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome, of which 6,789 families containing 20,739 genes appear to be <i>Echinochloa</i>-specific. On the basis of the orthologous single-copy genes, the <i>Oryza</i>- and <i>Sorghum</i>-<i>Echinochloa</i> divergence times were estimated to be ca. 48.5 and 28.5 million years ago (Mya), respectively (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig1">1a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Phylogenetics of <i>E. crus-galli</i>. <b>a</b> Single-copy gene based phylogenetic tree and divergence times of <i>E. crus-galli</i> and other grasses with Arabidopsis as an outgroup. <b>b</b> Distribution of the transversion rates at the four-fold degenerate sites (4dTv) of paralogous gene pairs of <i>E. crus-galli</i> and other grasses. The times of <i>E. crus-galli</i> speciation and <i>E. crus-galli</i>-<i>Sorghum</i> and -<i>Oryza</i> divergence are given based on corresponding paralogous and orthologous peaks</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>
                           <i>E. crus-galli</i> is an allohexaploid and therefore should be associated with ancient polyploidization events. We mapped <i>E. crus-galli</i> scaffolds to the diploid sorghum and foxtail millet (<i>S. italica</i>) genomes, and found that all chromosomes of sorghum and foxtail millet had three copies of <i>E. crus-galli</i> sequences aligned to them (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">3</a>), consistent with <i>E. crus-galli</i> being a hexaploid. We calculated the transversion rate at the four-fold degenerate sites (4dTv) of paralogous and orthologous gene pairs in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome. The orthologous peaks of <i>Echinochloa-Oryza</i> and <i>-Sorghum</i> had a corresponding divergence of 4dTvâââ0.27 and 0.16 and dated to 46.7 and 27.9âMya, respectively (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig1">1b</a>), which are consistent with the estimations based on the phylogenetic tree (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig1">1a</a>). The paralogous gene pairs based on syntenic blocks in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome showed one obvious large paralogous peak with 4dTvâââ0.042 dated the polyploidization event(s) for <i>E. crus-galli</i> speciation at ~â7.8âMya (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig1">1b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Gene families associated with detoxification</h3><p>Gene families such as cytochrome P450 monooxygenase (CYP450) and glutathione S-transferase (GST) are commonly associated with the detoxification of allelopathic compounds or non-target-site resistance to synthetic herbicides in weeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Yu, Q. &amp; Powles, S. Metabolism-based herbicide resistance and cross-resistance in crop weeds: A threat to herbicide sustainability and global crop production. Plant. Physiol. &#xA;                           166, 1106â1118 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1829">19</a></sup>. Additionally, some members of the CYP450 family are often involved in the secondary metabolism biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Boycheva, S., Daviet, L., Wolfender, J. &amp; Fitzpatrick, T. B. The rise of operon-like gene clusters in plants. Trends Plant. Sci. &#xA;                           19, 447â459 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1833">15</a></sup>. In the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome, a total of 917 CYP450 and 277 GST genes were identified, which is significantly higher than the numbers found in the genomes of the other five grasses (<i>O. sativa</i>, <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>, <i>S. italica</i>, <i>Z. mays</i>) used for comparison in this study and in Arabidopsis (249-354 CYP450 and 65-107 GST in a single genome). Enrichment analyses of Pfam domains showed that genes with the Pfam PF00067 (Cytochrome P450) and PF13417 (Glutathione S-transferase, N-terminal domain) are over-presented in <i>E. crus-galli</i> using other five grass species as background (PF00067: <i>P-</i>valueâ=â3.52E-9; PF13417: <i>P</i>-valueâ=â1.12E-3 both by hypergeometric test). Phylogenetic analysis indicates that the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome contains more diverse <i>E. crus-galli</i>-specific branches in the two gene families relative to rice (CYP450 as an example shown in Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">4</a>). In addition, we investigated divergence times of CYP450 and GST genes in <i>E. crus-galli</i> and rice, respectively. A significantly large peak was observed at the range of very low amino acid substitution rates for CYP450 and GST genes in <i>E. crus-galli</i>, indicating a recent expansion which was likely caused by polyploidization events generating a large number of CYP450 and GST genes in <i>E. crus-galli</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig2">2</a>). Transcriptomic profiles of <i>E. crus-galli</i> and rice plants involved in allelopathic interactions show that many genes from the two gene families are up-regulated (see below). These results suggest that the large repertoire of the CYP450 and GST genes may provide barnyardgrass enhanced detoxification ability under competition with nearby crop plants.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Gene families associated with detoxification in <i>E. crus-galli</i>. Distribution of amino acid substitute rates of <i>CYP450</i> and <i>GST</i> genes in <i>E. crus-galli</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> indicating a recent expansion of them in <i>E. crus-galli</i>
                                    </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Transcriptomic profiles of <i>E. crus-galli</i> allelopathy against rice</h3><p>To identify candidate genes involved in allelopathy of barnyardgrass against rice, the <i>E. crus-galli</i> line STB08 and a rice cultivar PI312777 (known to have a high allelopathic potential with barnyardgrass<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Dilday, R. H., Mattice, J. D., Moldenhauer, K. A. &amp; Yan, W. Allelopathic potential in rice germplasm against ducksalad, redstem and barnyard grass. J. Crop Prot. &#xA;                           4, 287â301 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1944">20</a></sup>) were grown separately (mono-cultured) or co-cultured (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">5</a>). Transcriptome analysis was performed on the barnyardgrass plants following 3âh of cultivation. Using transcriptomes of mono-cultured plants as reference, a total of 4945 differentially expressed genes (DEGs), including 2534 up-regulated and 2411 down-regulated genes, were identified. Pathway enrichment analyses using Mapman software suggested that the significantly down-regulated genes in co-cultivated barnyardgrass are involved in photosynthesis and tetrapyrrole synthesis, suggesting rice plants growing with the barnyardgrass may have imposed an allelopathic effect on the weed by inhibiting its photosynthetic gene expression (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig3">3a</a>). In contrast, pathways associated with âcytochrome P450â, âbrassinosteroid hormone metabolismâ, âphenylpropanoid metabolismâ were enriched and the majority of the DEGs were up-regulated in the co-cultured barnyardgrass (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig3">3b</a>; Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM3">1</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">6</a>). Phenylpropanoid metabolism in particular generates an enormous array of secondary metabolites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Vogt, T. Phenylpropanoid biosynthesis. Mol. Plant &#xA;                           3, 2â20 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1964">21</a></sup>. Brassinosteroids are phytohormones that have significant growth-promoting activity in plants and are involved in response to biotic and abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Bartwal, A., Mall, R., Lohani, P., Guru, S. K. &amp; Arora, S. Role of secondary metabolites and brassinosteroids in plant defense against environmental stresses. J. Plant. Growth. Regul. &#xA;                           32, 216â232 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d12320e1968">22</a></sup>. The identified up-regulated DEGs in these enriched pathways may confer critical response pathways allowing barnyardgrass to protect itself against rice during their allelopathic interactions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The allelopathic transcriptomic profiles of barnyardgrass against rice. <b>a</b> An overview for diverse pathways and <b>b</b> transcriptomic profile for large enzyme families visualized by Mapman. Color intensity corresponds to the expression fold change at log2 scale (red: up-regulated, blue: down-regulated)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">The gene clusters for biosynthesis of DIMBOA in <i>E. crus-galli</i>
                        </h3><p>The gene cluster for DIMBOA production has been described only in maize so far, although the presence of DIMBOA has been reported in other upland plants (such as wheat and rye)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sue, M., Nakamura, C. &amp; Nomura, T. Dispersed benzoxazinone gene cluster: molecular characterization and chromosomal localization of glucosyltransferase and glucosidase genes in wheat and rye. Plant. Physiol. &#xA;                           157, 985â997 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2009">23</a></sup>. Three copies of the DIMBOA gene cluster are found in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> STB08 genome each with perfect synteny with the maize genomic segment containing <i>BX1</i>-<i>5</i> and <i>BX8</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig4">4a</a>). Our transcriptome data indicated that almost all copies of these genes (with the exception of BX2 and BX8 in copy 2) in <i>E. crus-galli</i> were significantly up-regulated in barnyardgrass co-cultured with rice compared to mono-cultured STB08 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig4">4b</a>; Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM4">2</a>). To confirm that the induced expression of the DIMBOA biosynthetic gene cluster of barnyardgrass in response to co-cultivation with rice resulted in increased allelochemical production, we quantified the DIMBOA content in barnyardgrass. As shown in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig4">4c</a>, DIMBOA was significantly increased in barnyardgrass co-cultured with rice at all three time points (45âmin, 1.5 and 3âh). As expected, no DIMBOA was detected in rice control plants.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Characterization of biosynthetic gene clusters for DIMBOA in <i>E. crus-galli</i>. <b>a</b> Gene cluster for DIMBOA. Top, genomic synteny of the gene clusters for DIMBOA between barnyardgrass and maize. Bottom, the biosynthetic pathway of DIMBOA. <b>b</b> Expression changes of genes in the three copies of DIMBOA gene cluster in barnyardgrass during co-culture. Expression values were scaled for each gene. âMâ refers to mono-cultured experiments, and âCâ refers to experiments co-cultured with rice. <b>c</b> DIMBOA quantification in barnyardgrass. Left, selected HPLC ion chromatograms of root exudate extracts of barnyardgrass seedlings from mono-cultured (MC, as control) or co-cultured with rice (CC). Right, comparison of the DIMBOA concentrations in <i>E. crus-galli</i> from mono- and co-cultivated conditions at the specified time points. <b>d</b> Effect of DIMBOA treatment on the growth of rice seedlings. Top, phenotype of rice seedlings after one week treatment of 0.08 and 0.1âmM DIMBOA. Bottom, effects of DIMBOA treatment on plant height and fresh weight of rice seedlings</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Analysis of the effects of DIMBOA on rice growth showed that after DIMBOA treatment, rice plants showed a significant reduction in plant height and fresh weight. The inhibitory effects of DIMBOA were positively correlated DIMBOA concentration (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig4">4d</a>). Cumulatively these data suggest that the biosynthesis of DIMBOA by barnyardgrass is likely a significant mechanism in its competition with rice in the paddy field.</p><p>It is assumed that <i>E. crus-galli</i> (hexaploid) arises from the hybridization between tetraploid <i>E. oryzicola</i> (paternal donor) and an unknown diploid species (maternal donor)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Aoki, D. &amp; Yamaguchi, H. Genetic relationship between Echinochloa crus-galli and Echinochloa oryzicola accessions inferred from internal transcribed spacer and chloroplast DNA sequences. Weed Biol. Manag &#xA;                           8, 233â242 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2094">8</a></sup>. To determine the origins for the three copies of the DIMBOA gene clusters in <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome, we sequenced an <i>E. oryzicola</i> line (ZJU2, an estimated genome size of â~â1âGb) to â~â30âÃâcoverage (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">7</a>). Mapping results showed that scaffolds for two of the DIMBOA clusters (cluster copy 2 and 3) were well covered by the <i>E. oryzicola</i> reads, whereas the scaffold for cluster copy 1 could barely be mapped (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">7aâd</a>). These results indicate that the three copies of the DIMBOA cluster in the hexaploid species <i>E. crus-galli</i> were likely generated from a hybridization event, during which two DIMBOA copies were contributed by the tetraploid <i>E. oryzicola</i> while the unknown diploid species ancestor contribute to another DIMBOA copy.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">The gene cluster for biosynthesis of momilactone A in <i>E. crus-galli</i>
                        </h3><p>Interestingly, we also found a syntenic gene cluster in barnyardgrass corresponding to the gene cluster responsible for momilactone A biosynthesis in rice (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig5">5a</a>). Four adjoining genes in barnyardgrass were orthologous to four members of the gene cluster in rice despite some changes of gene order in the two genomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig5">5a</a>). However, the four genes in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> momilactone A biosynthesis gene cluster (only one copy found in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome) did not show any expression based on transcriptome analysis during barnyardgrass-rice co-cultivation. It has been previously reported that momilactones functions as both allelopathic and antimicrobial chemicals of rice against weeds and fungal pathogens, but can only exert little inhibition effect on the growth of seedlings of itself<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kato-Noguchi, H. &amp; Peters, R. J. The role of momilactones in rice allelopathy. J. Chem. Ecol. &#xA;                           39, 175â185 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2148">24</a></sup>. Besides, it has also been reported that momilactone A is significantly induced by blast (<i>P. oryzae</i>) infection of rice in paddy environments and serves as phytoalexin to enhance blast resistance in leaves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Hasegawa, M. et al. Phytoalexin accumulation in the interaction between rice and the blast fungus. Mol. Plant-Microbe Interact &#xA;                           23, 1000â1011 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2156">25</a></sup>. Therefore we hypothesized that the momilactone A cluster in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> was also associated with fungal resistance through the antimicrobial activity of momilactone A in the paddy filed. To test this directly, we infected STB08 leaves with <i>P. oryzae</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig5">5b</a>) and then examined the expression of the momilactone A synthase (<i>MAS</i>) and kaurene synthase-like (<i>KSL4</i>) genes in the momilactone A cluster using qRT-PCR. Our data show that the expression for the <i>MAS</i> and <i>KSL4</i> genes dramatically increased (in some cases exceeding 10-fold increases) after infection (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig5">5b</a>), indicating that the momilactone A gene cluster of <i>E. crus-galli</i> is activated by fungal stress and contributes to resistance to blast infection in paddy environment.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01067-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1067_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Characterization of the biosynthetic gene cluster for momilactone A in <i>E. crus-galli</i>. <b>a</b> Gene clusters responsible for biosynthesis of momilatone A in rice and barnyardgrass. Top, genomic synteny of the gene clusters for momilatone A between barnyardgrass and rice. Bottom, the biosynthetic pathway of momilatone A. Genomic positions of the gene clusters were adopted from Boycheva <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Boycheva, S., Daviet, L., Wolfender, J. &amp; Fitzpatrick, T. B. The rise of operon-like gene clusters in plants. Trends Plant. Sci. &#xA;                           19, 447â459 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2207">15</a></sup>. <b>b</b> Phenotypes of leaves and expression changes of genes in the momilactone A cluster of <i>E. crus-galli</i> under <i>P. oryzae</i> infection. Left, phenotypes for three leaves under <i>P. oryzae</i> infection and one as control. Right, gene expression changes for the three infected leaves, three technical replicates were performed for each leave. Expression value of genes without <i>P. oryzae</i> infection (control) was normalized to be one</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>As the momilactone A gene cluster was previously only found in <i>Oryza</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Miyamoto, K. et al. Evolutionary trajectory of phytoalexin biosynthetic gene clusters in rice. Plant. J. &#xA;                           87, 293â304 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2242">26</a></sup>, the identification of a momilactone gene cluster in <i>E. crus-galli</i> that is distantly related to that found in <i>O. sativa</i> raises questions as to its evolutionary origins. Therefore, we conducted a phylogenetic analysis of the genes in the momilactone cluster from <i>E. crus-galli</i> and those from other grasses. Interestingly, our analysis showed that the <i>E. crus-galli KSL4</i>, <i>CPS4</i>, and <i>MAS</i> first clustered with their orthologs from <i>Oryza</i> species and then other cereals (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">8</a>; Detailed gene labels see Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM5">3</a>). This clustering seems in contrast with the fact that <i>E. crus-galli</i> belongs to tribe Paniceae of the subfamily Panicoideae and near <i>S. italica</i> and <i>P. virgatum</i>, while <i>Oryza species</i> belong to the subfamily Oryzoideae (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#Fig1">1a</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">8a</a>). The tree for <i>CYP450</i> gene (<i>CYP99A2</i>) in the momilactone A cluster is consistent with the species phylogeny (i.e., they clustered with their orthologs in <i>Setaria/Panicum</i> first) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><p>We also checked whether the genomic segment harboring the momilactone A cluster in <i>E. crus-galli</i> could be mapped by reads from <i>E. oryzicola</i>. Almost no reads could be mapped on the genes for the cluster (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM1">7e</a>), suggesting that the momilactone A gene cluster in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome was not contributed by its paternal donor <i>E. oryzicola</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>We have generated the draft genome of the hexaploid species, <i>E. crus-galli</i>, one of the most notorious weeds known in modern crop fields. Our results provide new insights into the adaptive molecular mechanisms for their survival and invasiveness in the paddy field. Our data first shows that (1) <i>E. crus-galli</i> has evolved a large repertoire of detoxification-related genes (e.g., CYP450 and GST) and many of them are responsive during allelopathic interactions with rice; (2) <i>E. crus-galli</i> growing in paddy fields evolved multiple copies of the DIMBOA gene cluster which it uses in allelopathic interactions against rice and (3) also employs a momilactone A gene cluster against pathogenic fungi (and perhaps other biotic stresses) in the paddy field.</p><p>For polyploid species, sub-genomic sequence similarity is one of the challenges for genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ming, R. &amp; Wai, C. M. Assembling allopolyploid genomes: no longer formidable. Genome. Biol. &#xA;                           16, 27 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2345">27</a></sup>, as it may be difficult to distinguish two or more similar sub-genomes in a species, which could possibly introduce mis-assembly and can influence following utility of the draft genome. Luckily, it seems that the divergence level among sub-genomes of allopolyploid species (e.g., tobacco<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Sierro, N. et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat. Commun. &#xA;                           5, 3833 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2349">28</a></sup>, cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2353">29</a></sup>, rapeseed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science &#xA;                           345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2357">30</a></sup>, and also <i>E. crus-galli</i> in this case) is high enough, and classical short sequencing data based assembly tools, such as SOAPdenovo and ALLPATHS-LG, can assemble their genomes well to a comparable assembly statistics (such as scaffold N50) to that of many diploid genomes. Currently, third generation sequencing (TGS) technologies have been comprehensively used in genome assembly. However, the polyploidy genome features could pose more challenges for utilization these data. It should be noted that the average sequencing error rate of TGS is still very high (â~â15%), which could be similar or even higher than the sequence differences between the sub-genomes of polyploidy species. Therefore, pre-correction step for TGS reads are quite necessary for using these data. In addition, we found that in our case, only less than 6âGb corrected Pacbio data (efficiency &lt;â20%) is left after correction by Canu, which may indicate that more raw data are needed for polyploidy genome sequencing projects.</p><p>Biosynthetic gene clusters responsible for known allelopathic compounds and phytoalexin (DIMBOA and momilactones) were found in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome, which have been identified in crops (such as maize and rice). Several benefits of gene clusters include improved efficiency in functionality, heredity, and survival compared with non-clustered genes functioning in the same pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Boycheva, S., Daviet, L., Wolfender, J. &amp; Fitzpatrick, T. B. The rise of operon-like gene clusters in plants. Trends Plant. Sci. &#xA;                           19, 447â459 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2370">15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Takos, A. M. &amp; Rook, F. Why biosynthetic genes for chemical defense compounds cluster. Trends Plant. Sci. &#xA;                           17, 383â388 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2373">31</a></sup>. These evolved functional gene clusters may imply one of the critical genetic bases for rapid environmental adaptability of the weed. For example, our results demonstrated that DIMBOA is an essential allelochemical employed by barnyardgrass to inhibit rice growth in paddy fields. <i>E. crus-galli</i> have evolved to form multiple copies of DIMBOA gene cluster, which should play a critical role in competition with rice and its invasiveness in paddy fields. Further, as to implications for rice breeding, it is crucial to find genes or loci responsible for DIMBOA resistance in rice genome, and rice cultivars with improved DIMBOA resistance may be produced through molecular breeding in the future.</p><p>The syntenic gene cluster for momilactone A has previously only been found in the rice genome. On the basis our transcriptome profiling analysis of baryardgrass and rice plants under co-cultivation, it does not appear that the momilactone A gene cluster in <i>E. crus-galli</i> is activated during these interactions. While the exact role(s) of the momilactone A cluster in <i>E. crus-galli</i> still requires further investigations, it appears that it may be involved in regulating biotic stress, primarily in response to infection of <i>P. oryzae</i>, a significant constraint on growth in rice paddies. The evolved momilactone A gene cluster in <i>E. crus-galli</i> could enhance its adaptability in paddy environment.</p><p>In investigation of the evolutionary history of momilactone A cluster, we only found one copy of momilactone A gene cluster in <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome, and it appears that the tetraploid paternal species <i>E. oryzicola</i> does not harbor this cluster or the related genes. Several reasonable explanations can be put forth to account for this observation. First, the unknown diploid <i>Echinochloa</i> species itself equips the momilactone A cluster, and contribute the cluster to <i>E. crus-galli</i> during hybridization with <i>E. oryzicola</i>. Alternatively, the diploid species does not harbor the cluster but has the related genes. After hybridization, the cluster formed in the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome with genomic rearrangements of genes (<i>CPS4</i>, <i>KSL4</i>, <i>MAS</i>, and <i>CYP99A</i>) involved in the biosynthetic pathway. The unknown diploid <i>Echinochloa</i> species material is crucial for better understanding of the evolutionary mystery of the momilactone A gene cluster, and the environmental adaptation of <i>Echinochloa</i> weeds. Our phylogenetic analyses showed that genes of <i>E. crus-galli</i> in the momilactone A gene cluster seems closer to <i>O. sativa</i> than other phylogenetically close species (e.g., <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>Z. latifolia</i> or even <i>O. brachyantha</i>). The unexpected phylogenetic distributions suggested that complex processes (e.g., multiple gene duplications followed by different losses, or even horizontal gene transfer) could be involved in the evolution of the genes (<i>CPS4</i>, <i>KSL4</i>, <i>MAS</i>) and the formation of the momilactone gene cluster in <i>E. crus-galli</i>, which need further investigation in future.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>The <i>E. crus-galli</i> line STB08, collected from rice paddy fields in the lower Yangtze River region of China (30Â°17â² N, 119Â°57â² E). Genomic DNA was extracted from young leaves of <i>E. crus-galli</i> (STB08) plants using the CTAB method. RNase A and proteinase K were used to remove RNA and protein contamination, respectively. Paired-end and mate-pair Illumina genomic DNA libraries with different insertion sizes (160âbpâ20âKb) were constructed following the manufacturerâs instructions (Illumina, USA). The libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 system. Meanwhile, two Pacbio RS II P4-C2 libraries of STB08 were constructed following the manufacturerâs instructions and 48 SMRT cells (32.9âGb) were sequenced. Raw reads were processed by removing PCR duplicates, low-quality reads, adaptor sequences and contaminated reads with bacterial or viral origin. In addition, we used Lighter software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Song, L., Florea, L. &amp; Langmead, B. Lighter: fast and memory-efficient sequencing error correction without counting. Genome. Biol. &#xA;                           15, 509 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2483">32</a></sup> to correct the reads from each library based on the <i>K</i>-mer frequency. The resulting clean reads were assembled into contigs and scaffolds using SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience &#xA;                           1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2490">33</a></sup> with the default settings. The assembled sequences were further scaffolded with OPERA-LG v2.0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gao, S., Bertrand, D., Chia, B. K. H. &amp; Nagarajan, N. OPERA-LG: efficient and exact scaffolding of large, repeat-rich eukaryotic genomes with performance guarantees. Genome. Biol. &#xA;                           17, 102 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2495">34</a></sup>, and gaps within the scaffolds were filled using GapCloser v1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience &#xA;                           1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2499">33</a></sup>. Redundans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Pryszcz, L. P. &amp; Gabaldon, T. Redundans: an assembly pipeline for highly heterozygous genomes. Nucleic Acids. Res. &#xA;                           44, e113 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2503">35</a></sup> was used to filter redundant sequences due to heterozygosity. Raw Pacbio RS long reads were corrected by Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. &#xA;                           27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2507">36</a></sup>, and then were used to fill the gaps by PBjelly v14.1with the default settings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE &#xA;                           7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2511">37</a></sup>. Detailed assembly pipeline and statistics are shown in Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#MOESM6">4</a>, respectively. Both CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2519">38</a></sup> and BUSCO v2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2523">39</a></sup> were used to evaluate the completeness of assembled genome. Fosmid libraries were prepared following the manual of CopyControl Fosmid Library Production Kit (Epicentre, Madison, WI). In addition, 74 publicly available <i>E. crus-galli</i> EST sequences and 156,757 transcripts generated by this study were aligned to the assembled genome by GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           21, 1859â1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2530">40</a></sup> for validation. For <i>E. oryzicola</i> line ZJU2 (collected from 23Â°31â²N, 111Â°43â²E), paired-end DNA libraries (800âbp) were prepared and sequenced by HiSeq2500. Clean reads were mapped to the assembled <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome by Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods &#xA;                           9, 357â359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2541">41</a></sup> and the mapping result was visualized using inGAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Qi, J., Zhao, F., Buboltz, A. &amp; Schuster, S. C. inGAP: an integrated next-generation genome analysis pipeline. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           26, 127â129 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2545">42</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Genome gene and repeat annotation</h3><p>We constructed an <i>E. crus-galli</i> repeat library using RepeatModeler v1.0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           Chapter 4, Unit 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2560">43</a></sup> with the default parameters. Two complementary programs (RECON and RepeatScout) were configured in RepeatModeler and were used for identification of repeat family sequences in the genome. The resulting <i>E. crus-galli</i> repeat library was further used to run RepeatMasker v3.1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           Chapter 4, Unit 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2567">43</a></sup> for the whole genome repeat annotation.</p><p>A hybrid strategy combining ab initio predictions, homologous gene evidence and transcriptomic support (RNA-seq) was applied in gene prediction. Three ab initio gene finders, GeneMark.hmm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Lukashin, A. V. &amp; Borodovsky, M. GeneMark.hmm: new solutions for gene finding. Nucleic. Acids. Res. &#xA;                           26, 1107â1115 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2574">44</a></sup>, Fgenesh<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. &#xA;                           10, 516â522 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2578">45</a></sup>, and Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic. Acids. Res. &#xA;                           34, W435âW439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2582">46</a></sup> were used. Protein sequences of three closely related species (<i>Z. mays</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>, <i>S. italica</i>) were aligned to the assembled <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome using Spaln2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Iwata, H. &amp; Gotoh, O. Benchmarking spliced alignment programs including Spaln2, an extended version of Spaln that incorporates additional species-specific features. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           40, e161 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2599">47</a></sup> to get evidences of the gene structure. RNA-seq data generated from the whole plant of <i>E. crus-galli</i> were used for gene annotation. The reads were <i>de novo</i> assembled by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2609">48</a></sup> into raw transcripts. The Seqclean utility implemented in PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic. Acids. Res. &#xA;                           31, 5654â5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2614">49</a></sup> was applied to identify evidence of polyadenylation, strip the poly-As, trim vectors, and to discard low-quality sequences. The remaining transcripts were aligned to the <i>E. crus-galli</i> genome using GMAP. The valid transcript alignments were clustered and further assembled by PASA based on genomic location and then the transcriptome-based consensus gene models were generated. All gene structures predicted by the above procedures were integrated into consensus gene models by EVM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using evidence modeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome. Biol. &#xA;                           9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2621">50</a></sup>. The predicted genes were then checked manually. The final set of genes were determined using the following criteria: (1) Partial gene models without start or stop codons were removed; (2) No âNâs residing in the coding sequences (CDS); (3) Sequences homologous with the Repbase (E-valueââ¤â1e-5, identityââ¥â30%, coverageââ¥â30% and minimum matching lengthââ¥â30 aa) were removed.</p><p>The predicted <i>E. crus-galli</i> genes were aligned against non-redundant green plant protein databases in Swiss-Prot and NR protein databases for functional annotation (BLASTP, E-valueââ¤â1e-5). Protein domains were predicted by comparing the sequences against various domain databases, including Pfam, TIGRFAM, ProDom, and SMART using InterProScan v5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScanâan integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           17, 847â848 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01067-5#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d12320e2631">51</a></sup>. Gene ontology (GO) terms for each gene were assigned based on corresponding InterPro entries.
